=100  \\$aTiton du Tillet, Évrard$d1677-1762$4aut$1http://www.wikidata.org/entity/Q3592904$97631
=245  \\$aVies des Musiciens et autres Joueurs d'Instruments du règne de Louis le Grand
=260  \\$aParis$bLe Promeneur$c1991
=300  \\$a141 pages
=520  \\$aCe livre est le recueil des anecdotes ou des légendes qui coururent sur les musiciens français sous le règne de Louis XIV. Titon du Tillet fait revivre les derniers joueurs de luth, la naissance de l'opéra français à machines. Il fait revivre Lully en carrosse, en grand chapeau de castor, entendant chanter ses airs au coin des rues. Il fait revivre Delalanche brisant son violon dans un accès de colère, ou bien le jeune Marin Marais se glissant sous la cabane de Sainte-Colombe, édifiée dans un mûrier. C'est le texte source de la musique française du XVIIe siècle.

=041  \\$aita
=245  03$aLa ceramica degli Antonibon
=260  \\$aMilan$bElecta$c1990
=300  \\$a200 pages
=500  \\$aLe Nove porcelain was made in the 18th century in the town now called Nove, near Bassano, then in the Republic of Venice's mainland territories, the terrafirma. It was made at a factory owned by Pasquale Antonibon, who was already making fine maiolica in fashionable styles, which continued to be made alongside the porcelain. Production of porcelain began in 1762 and ended when Antonibon died in 1773. But it resumed in 1781, when Francisco Parolin (or Parolini) leased the factory for twenty years in a partnership with the Antonibons, known as the "Parolin period".This lasted until 1802.Production of porcelain continued intermittently until 1835. (Wikipedia)

=100  1\$aGulik, Willem R. van$4edt$941582
=245  \\$aIn het spoor van de liefde:$bJapans-Nederlandse ontmoetingen sinds 1600
=260  \\$c1986$aAmsterdam$bDe Bataafse Leeuw
=300  \\$a206 pages
=500  \\$aThis publication accompanies the exhibition 'In het Spoor van de Liefde' held at the 'Museum voor Volkenkunde' from 6 September 1986 to 5 January 1987.
=520  \\$aThis book examines the cultural relations that developed between the Netherlands and Japan in the wake of their commercial ties. From economic, social and cultural-historical perspectives, the authors recount the story of the deepening interest that the Japanese and Dutch developed in each other's cultures. As intensive as trade relations with Japan are now, they were minimal in the seventeenth century. Japan's self-imposed isolation virtually ruled out trade with the non-Japanese world. The umbilical cord connecting the Land of the Rising Sun with the rest of the world was formed by a Dutch trading settlement on the island of Deshima in Nagasaki Bay. It would take until 1853 before the isolation was broken by the American navy under the command of Commander Perry. The mutual influence between the Netherlands and Japan remained strong afterwards. (Translated with DeepL.com)
